      <p>
       <b>
        Quechua
       </b>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">
            ɛ
           </span>
           <span title="/tʃ/: 'ch' in 'China'">
            tʃ
           </span>
           <span title="'w' in 'wind'">
            w
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       , known as
       <i>
        <b>
         Runasimi
        </b>
       </i>
       ("people's language") in the Quechuan language, is an
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">
        indigenous language family
       </a>
       , with variations spoken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Quechua_people" title="Quechua people">
        Quechua peoples
       </a>
       , primarily living in the
       <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">
        Andes
       </a>
       and highlands of
       <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
        South America
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Derived from a common ancestral language, it is the most widely spoken language family of
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">
        indigenous peoples of the Americas
       </a>
       , with a total of probably some 8–10 million speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelaar167_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelaar167-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Approximately 25% (7.7 million) of Peruvians speak some variation of Quechua.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is perhaps most widely known for being the main language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">
        Inca Empire
       </a>
       . The colonisers initially encouraged its use, but from the middle of their reign they suppressed it. However, Quechua ultimately
       <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">
        survived
       </a>
       , and variants are still widely spoken today.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Quechua had already expanded across wide ranges of the central Andes long before the expansion of the
       <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">
        Inca Empire
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The Inca were one among many peoples in present-day Peru who already spoke forms of Quechua. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">
        Cusco
       </a>
       region, Quechua was influenced by local languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">
        Aymara
       </a>
       . The Cuzco variety of Quechua developed as quite distinct. In similar ways, diverse dialects developed in different areas, related to existing local languages, when the Inca Empire ruled and imposed Quechua as the official language.
      </p>
      <p>
       After the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Inca_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire">
        Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire
       </a>
       in the 16th century, Quechua continued to be used widely by the indigenous peoples as the "common language". It was officially recognized by the Spanish administration and many Spanish learned it in order to communicate with the local peoples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Clergy of the
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic Church
       </a>
       adopted Quechua to use as the language of
       <a href="/wiki/Evangelism" title="Evangelism">
        evangelization
       </a>
       . Given its use by the Catholic missionaries, the range of Quechua continued to expand in some areas.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the late 18th century, colonial officials ended administrative and religious use of Quechua, banning it from public use in Peru after the
       <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BApac_Amaru_II" title="Túpac Amaru II">
        Túpac Amaru II
       </a>
       rebellion of indigenous peoples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelaar167_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelaar167-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Crown banned even "loyal" pro-Catholic texts in Quechua, such as Garcilaso de la Vega's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Comentarios_Reales_de_los_Incas" title="Comentarios Reales de los Incas">
         Comentarios Reales
        </a>
        .
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite a brief revival of the language immediately after the Latin American nations achieved independence in the 19th century, the prestige of Quechua had decreased sharply. Gradually its use declined so that it was spoken mostly by indigenous people in the more isolated and conservative rural areas. Nevertheless, in the 21st century, those speaking Quechua language speakers number 8 to 10 million people across South America,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelaar167_5-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelaar167-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the most speakers of any indigenous language.
      </p>
      <p>
       The oldest written records of the language are by missionary
       <a href="/wiki/Domingo_de_Santo_Tom%C3%A1s" title="Domingo de Santo Tomás">
        Domingo de Santo Tomás
       </a>
       , who arrived in Peru in 1538 and learned the language from 1540. He published his
       <i>
        Grammatica o arte de la lengua general de los indios de los reynos del Perú
       </i>
       (Grammar or Art of the General Language of the Indians of the Royalty of Peru) in 1560.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">
        Current status
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Current status">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:El_quechua_como_lengua_materna_(censo_nacional_2007).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="1632" height="453" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/El_quechua_como_lengua_materna_%28censo_nacional_2007%29.png/350px-El_quechua_como_lengua_materna_%28censo_nacional_2007%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/El_quechua_como_lengua_materna_%28censo_nacional_2007%29.png/525px-El_quechua_como_lengua_materna_%28censo_nacional_2007%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/El_quechua_como_lengua_materna_%28censo_nacional_2007%29.png/700px-El_quechua_como_lengua_materna_%28censo_nacional_2007%29.png 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:El_quechua_como_lengua_materna_(censo_nacional_2007).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map of Peru showing the distribution of overall Quechua speakers by district
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1975 Peru became the first country to recognize Quechua as one of its official languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT1975_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT1975-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ecuador conferred official status on the language in its 2006 constitution, and in 2009, Bolivia adopted a new constitution that recognized Quechua and several other indigenous languages as official languages of the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andes_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Andes-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The major obstacle to the usage and teaching of Quechua is the lack of written materials in that language, such as books, newspapers, software, and magazines. The Bible has been translated into Quechua and is distributed by certain missionary groups. Quechua, along with
       <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">
        Aymara
       </a>
       and the minor indigenous languages, remains essentially a
       <a href="/wiki/Spoken_language" title="Spoken language">
        spoken language
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In recent years, Quechua has been introduced in
       <a href="/wiki/Intercultural_bilingual_education" title="Intercultural bilingual education">
        intercultural bilingual education
       </a>
       (IBE) in
       <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">
        Bolivia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">
        Ecuador
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
        Peru
       </a>
       . Even in these areas, the governments are reaching only a part of the Quechua-speaking populations. Some indigenous people in each of the countries are having their children study in Spanish for the purposes of social advancement.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_Nacional_del_Per%C3%BA" title="Radio Nacional del Perú">
        Radio Nacional del Perú
       </a>
       broadcasts news and agrarian programs in Quechua for periods in the mornings.
      </p>
      <p>
       Quechua and Spanish are now heavily intermixed in much of the Andean region, with many hundreds of Spanish loanwords in Quechua. Conversely, Quechua phrases and words are commonly used by Spanish speakers. In southern rural Bolivia, for instance, many Quechua words such as
       <i>
        wawa
       </i>
       (infant),
       <i>
        misi
       </i>
       (cat),
       <i>
        waska
       </i>
       (strap or thrashing), are as commonly used as their Spanish counterparts, even in entirely Spanish-speaking areas. Quechua has also had a profound influence on other native languages of the Americas, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Mapuche_language" title="Mapuche language">
        Mapuche
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_speakers">
        Number of speakers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Number of speakers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The number of speakers given varies widely according to the sources. The total in
       <i>
        Ethnologue
       </i>
       16 is 10 million, mostly based on figures published 1987–2002, but with a few dating from the 1960s. The figure for Imbabura Highland Quechua in
       <i>
        Ethnologue
       </i>
       , for example, is 300,000, an estimate from 1977.
      </p>
      <p>
       The missionary organization FEDEPI, on the other hand, estimated one million Imbabura dialect speakers (published 2006). Census figures are also problematic, due to under-reporting. The 2001 Ecuador census reports only 500,000 Quechua speakers, compared to the estimate in most linguistic sources of more than 2 million. The censuses of Peru (2007) and Bolivia (2001) are thought to be more reliable.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Argentina: 900,000 (1971)
       </li>
       <li>
        Bolivia: 2,100,000 (2001 census); 2,800,000 South Bolivian (1987)
       </li>
       <li>
        Chile: few, if any; 8200 in ethnic group (2002 census)
       </li>
       <li>
        Colombia: 4,402 to 16,000
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
         <a href="#cite_note-15">
          [15]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Ecuador: 2,300,000 (Adelaar 1991)
       </li>
       <li>
        Peru: 3,260,000 (2007 census); 3,500,000 to 4,400,000 (Adelaar 2000)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Additionally, there is an unknown number of speakers in emigrant communities, including
       <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">
        Queens
       </a>
       , New York, and
       <a href="/wiki/Paterson,_New_Jersey" title="Paterson, New Jersey">
        Paterson, New Jersey
       </a>
       , in the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quechua_(subgrupos).svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Quechua_%28subgrupos%29.svg/220px-Quechua_%28subgrupos%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Quechua_%28subgrupos%29.svg/330px-Quechua_%28subgrupos%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Quechua_%28subgrupos%29.svg/440px-Quechua_%28subgrupos%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quechua_(subgrupos).svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The four branches of Quechua: I (Central), II-A (North Peruvian), II-B (Northern), II-C (Southern)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are significant differences among the varieties of Quechua spoken in the central Peruvian highlands and the peripheral varieties of Ecuador, as well as those of southern Peru and Bolivia. They can be labeled Quechua I (or Quechua B, central) and Quechua II (or Quechua A, peripheral). Within the two groups, there are few sharp boundaries, making them
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">
        dialect continua
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       But, there is a secondary division in Quechua II between the grammatically simplified northern varieties of Ecuador, Quechua II-B, known there as
       <a href="/wiki/Kichwa_language" title="Kichwa language">
        Kichwa
       </a>
       , and the generally more conservative varieties of the southern highlands, Quechua II-C, which include the old Inca capital of
       <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">
        Cusco
       </a>
       . The closeness is at least in part because of the influence of
       <a href="/wiki/Cusco_Quechua" title="Cusco Quechua">
        Cusco Quechua
       </a>
       on the Ecuadorean varieties in the Inca Empire. Because Northern nobles were required to educate their children in Cusco, this was maintained as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prestige_dialect" title="Prestige dialect">
        prestige dialect
       </a>
       in the north.
      </p>
      <p>
       Speakers from different points within any of the three regions can generally understand one another reasonably well. There are nonetheless significant local-level differences across each. (
       <a href="/wiki/Wanka_Quechua" title="Wanka Quechua">
        Wanka Quechua
       </a>
       , in particular, has several very distinctive characteristics that make the variety more difficult to understand, even for other Central Quechua speakers.) Speakers from different major regions, particularly Central or Southern Quechua, are not able to communicate effectively.
      </p>
      <p>
       The lack of
       <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">
        mutual intelligibility
       </a>
       among the dialects is the basic criterion that defines Quechua not as a single language, but as a language family. The complex and progressive nature of how speech varies across the dialect continua makes it nearly impossible to differentiate discrete varieties;
       <i>
        Ethnologue
       </i>
       lists 45 varieties which are then divided into 2 groups; Central and Peripheral. Due to the non-intelligibility among the 2 groups, they are all classified as separate languages..
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As a reference point, the overall degree of diversity across the family is a little less than that of the
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       families, and more of the order of
       <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">
        Slavic
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       . The greatest diversity is within Central Quechua,
       <span class="smallcaps">
        <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
         AKA
        </span>
       </span>
       Quechua I, which is believed to lie close to the homeland of the ancestral Proto-Quechua language.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Family_tree">
        Family tree
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Family tree">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Alfredo_Torero" title="Alfredo Torero">
        Alfredo Torero
       </a>
       devised the traditional classification, the three divisions above, plus a fourth, a northern or Peruvian branch. The latter causes complications in the classification, however, as the northern dialects (
       <a href="/wiki/Cajamarca%E2%80%93Ca%C3%B1aris_Quechua" title="Cajamarca–Cañaris Quechua">
        Cajamarca–Cañaris
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pacaraos_Quechua" title="Pacaraos Quechua">
        Pacaraos
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Yauyos%E2%80%93Chincha_Quechua" title="Yauyos–Chincha Quechua">
        Yauyos–Chincha
       </a>
       ) have features of both Quechua I and Quechua II, and so are difficult to assign to either.
      </p>
      <p>
       Torero classifies them as the following:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_I" title="Quechua I">
         Quechua I
        </a>
        or
        <i>
         Quechua B,
        </i>
        aka
        <i>
         Central Quechua
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         Waywash,
        </i>
        spoken in Peru's central highlands and coast.
        <ul>
         <li>
          The most widely spoken varieties are Huaylas, Huaylla Wanca, and Conchucos.
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_II" title="Quechua II">
         Quechua II
        </a>
        or
        <i>
         Quechua A
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         Peripheral Quechua
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         Wanp'una
        </i>
        , divided into
        <ul>
         <li>
          Yungay (Yunkay) Quechua or
          <i>
           Quechua II A,
          </i>
          spoken in the northern mountains of Peru; the most widely spoken dialect is Cajamarca.
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Quechua" title="Northern Quechua">
           Northern Quechua
          </a>
          or
          <i>
           Quechua II B,
          </i>
          spoken in Ecuador (
          <a href="/wiki/Kichwa_language" title="Kichwa language">
           Kichwa
          </a>
          ), northern Peru, and Colombia (
          <a href="/wiki/Inga_Kichwa" title="Inga Kichwa">
           Inga Kichwa
          </a>
          )
          <ul>
           <li>
            The most widely spoken varieties in this group are Chimborazo Highland Quichua and Imbabura Highland Quichua.
           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Southern_Quechua" title="Southern Quechua">
           Southern Quechua
          </a>
          or
          <i>
           Quechua II C,
          </i>
          spoken in Bolivia, southern Peru, Chile, and Argentina.
          <ul>
           <li>
            The most widely spoken varieties are South Bolivian, Cusco, Ayacucho, and Puno (Collao).
           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Willem_Adelaar" title="Willem Adelaar">
        Willem Adelaar
       </a>
       adheres to the Quechua I / Quechua II (central/peripheral) bifurcation. But, partially following later modifications by Torero, he reassigns part of Quechua II-A to Quechua I:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

                     <p>
                      <a href="/wiki/South_Bolivian_Quechua" title="South Bolivian Quechua">
                       Southern Bolivia
                      </a>
                     </p>
                    </td>
                   </tr>
                   <tr>
                    <td style="border:0;border-left:1px solid;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;">
                     <br/>
                    </td>
                   </tr>
                   <tr>
                    <td style="border:0;padding:0 0.2em;border-left:1px solid;border-bottom:1px solid;vertical-align:bottom;text-align:center;">
                     <br/>
                    </td>
                    <td rowspan="2" style="border:0;padding:0;">
                     <p>
                      <a href="/wiki/Santiague%C3%B1o_Quechua" title="Santiagueño Quechua">
                       Santiago del Estero
                      </a>
                     </p>
                    </td>
                   </tr>
                   <tr>
                    <td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;">
                     <br/>
                    </td>
                   </tr>
                  </table>
                 </td>
                </tr>
                <tr>
                 <td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;">
                  <br/>
                 </td>
                </tr>
               </table>
              </td>
             </tr>
             <tr>
              <td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;">
               <br/>
              </td>
             </tr>
            </table>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
           <td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;">
            <br/>
           </td>
          </tr>
         </table>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;">
         <br/>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Landerman (1991) does not believe a truly genetic classification is possible and divides Quechua II so that the family has four geographical–typological branches: Northern, North Peruvian, Central, and Southern. He includes Chachapoyas and Lamas in North Peruvian Quechua so Ecuadorian is synonymous with Northern Quechua.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_distribution">
        Geographical distribution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geographical distribution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Quechua I (Central Quechua,
       <i>
        Waywash
       </i>
       ) is spoken in Peru's central highlands, from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancash_Region" title="Ancash Region">
        Ancash Region
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Huancayo" title="Huancayo">
        Huancayo
       </a>
       . It is the most diverse branch of Quechua,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to the extent that its divisions are commonly considered different languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       Quechua II (Peripheral Quechua,
       <i>
        Wamp'una
       </i>
       "Traveler")
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        II-A: Yunkay Quechua (North Peruvian Quechua) is scattered in Peru's occidental highlands
       </li>
       <li>
        II-B: Northern Quechua (also known as
        <i>
         Runashimi
        </i>
        or, especially in Ecuador,
        <a href="/wiki/Kichwa_language" title="Kichwa language">
         <i>
          Kichwa
         </i>
        </a>
        ) is mainly spoken in Colombia and Ecuador. It is also spoken in the Amazonian lowlands of Colombia, Ecuador, and in pockets in Peru
       </li>
       <li>
        II-C:
        <a href="/wiki/Southern_Quechua" title="Southern Quechua">
         Southern Quechua
        </a>
        , in the highlands further south, from
        <a href="/wiki/Huancavelica" title="Huancavelica">
         Huancavelica
        </a>
        through the
        <a href="/wiki/Ayacucho" title="Ayacucho">
         Ayacucho
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">
         Cusco
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Puno" title="Puno">
         Puno
        </a>
        regions of
        <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
         Peru
        </a>
        , across much of
        <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">
         Bolivia
        </a>
        , and in pockets in north-western
        <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
         Argentina
        </a>
        . It is the most influential branch, with the largest number of speakers and the most important cultural and literary legacy.
       </li>
      </ul>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Quechua_and_Aymara">
        Quechua and Aymara
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Quechua and Aymara">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Quechua shares a large amount of vocabulary, and some striking structural parallels, with
       <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">
        Aymara
       </a>
       , and the two families have sometimes been grouped together as a "
       <a href="/wiki/Quechumaran_languages" title="Quechumaran languages">
        Quechumaran family
       </a>
       ". That hypothesis is generally rejected by specialists, however. The parallels are better explained by mutual influence and borrowing through intensive and long-term contact. Many Quechua–Aymara cognates are close, often closer than intra-Quechua cognates, and there is little relationship in the
       <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">
        affixal
       </a>
       system.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">
        Vocabulary
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       A number of Quechua
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       have entered
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       via
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">
         coca
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cockroach" title="Cockroach">
         cockroach
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Condor" title="Condor">
         condor
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">
         guano
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jerky" title="Jerky">
         jerky
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">
         llama
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pampa" title="Pampa">
         pampa
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Poncho" title="Poncho">
         poncho
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Puma_(genus)" title="Puma (genus)">
         puma
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">
         quinine
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Quinoa" title="Quinoa">
         quinoa
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Vicu%C3%B1a" title="Vicuña">
         vicuña
        </a>
       </i>
       , and, possibly,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gaucho" title="Gaucho">
         gaucho
        </a>
       </i>
       . The word
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lagniappe" title="Lagniappe">
         lagniappe
        </a>
       </i>
       comes from the Quechuan word
       <i>
        yapay
       </i>
       ("to increase; to add") with the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       article
       <i>
        la
       </i>
       in front of it,
       <i>
        la yapa
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        la ñapa
       </i>
       in Spanish.
      </p>
      <p>
       The influence on Latin American Spanish includes such borrowings as
       <i>
        papa
       </i>
       for "potato",
       <i>
        chuchaqui
       </i>
       for "hangover" in
       <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">
        Ecuador
       </a>
       , and diverse borrowings for "
       <a href="/wiki/Altitude_sickness" title="Altitude sickness">
        altitude sickness
       </a>
       ", in
       <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">
        Bolivia
       </a>
       from Quechuan
       <i>
        suruqch'i
       </i>
       to Bolivian
       <i>
        sorojchi
       </i>
       , in
       <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">
        Ecuador
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
        Peru
       </a>
       <i>
        soroche
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In Bolivia, particularly, Quechua words are used extensively even by non-Quechua speakers. These include wawa (baby, infant), ch'aki (hangover), misi (cat), juk'ucho (mouse), q'omer uchu (green pepper), jacu ("lets go"), chhiri and chhurco (curly haired), among many others. Quechua grammar also enters Bolivian Spanish, such as the use of the suffix -ri. In Bolivian Quechua, -ri is added to verbs to signify an action is performed with affection or, in the imperative, as a rough equivalent to please. In Bolivia -ri is often included in the Spanish imperative to imply "please" or to soften commands. For example, the standard "pásame" (pass me), becomes pasarime.
      </p>
      <p>
       Quechua has borrowed a large number of
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       words, such as
       <i>
        piru
       </i>
       (from
       <i>
        pero
       </i>
       , but),
       <i>
        bwenu
       </i>
       (from
       <i>
        bueno
       </i>
       , good), iskwila (from "escuela," school), waka (from "vaca," cow) and
       <i>
        burru
       </i>
       (from
       <i>
        burro
       </i>
       , donkey).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_of_Quechua">
        Etymology of Quechua
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Etymology of Quechua">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       At first, Spaniards referred to the language of the Inca empire as the
       <i>
        lengua general
       </i>
       , the
       <i>
        general language
       </i>
       . The name
       <i>
        quichua
       </i>
       is first used in 1560 by
       <a href="/wiki/Domingo_de_Santo_Tom%C3%A1s" title="Domingo de Santo Tomás">
        Domingo de Santo Tomás
       </a>
       in his
       <i>
        Grammatica o arte de la lengua general de los indios de los reynos del Perú
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adelaar179_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Adelaar179-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is not known what name the native speakers gave to their language before colonial times and whether it was Spaniards who called it
       <i>
        quechua
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adelaar179_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Adelaar179-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are two possible etymologies of Quechua as the name of the language. There is a possibility that the name Quechua was derived from
       <i>
        *qiĉ.wa
       </i>
       , the native word which originally meant the "temperate valley" altitude ecological zone in the Andes (suitable for maize cultivation) and to its inhabitants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adelaar179_22-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Adelaar179-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Alternatively,
       <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Cieza_de_Le%C3%B3n" title="Pedro Cieza de León">
        Pedro Cieza de León
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Inca_Garcilaso_de_la_Vega" title="Inca Garcilaso de la Vega">
        Inca Garcilaso de la Vega
       </a>
       , the early Spanish chroniclers, mention the existence of a people called Quichua in the present
       <a href="/wiki/Apur%C3%ADmac_Region" title="Apurímac Region">
        Apurímac Region
       </a>
       , and it could be inferred that their name was given to the entire language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adelaar179_22-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Adelaar179-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Hispanicised spellings
       <i>
        Quechua
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Quichua
       </i>
       have been used in Peru and Bolivia since the 17th century, especially after the
       <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Lima" title="Third Council of Lima">
        Third Council of Lima
       </a>
       . Today, the various local pronunciations of "Quechua Simi" include
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Quechua" title="Help:IPA/Quechua">
         [ˈqʰeʃwa ˈsimi]
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Quechua" title="Help:IPA/Quechua">
         [ˈχetʃwa ˈʃimi]
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Quechua" title="Help:IPA/Quechua">
         [ˈkitʃwa ˈʃimi]
        </a>
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Quechua" title="Help:IPA/Quechua">
         [ˈʔitʃwa ˈʃimi]
        </a>
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Another name that native speakers give to their own language is
       <i>
        runa simi
       </i>
       , "language of man/people"; it also seems to have emerged during the colonial period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adelaar179_22-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Adelaar179-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The description below applies to
       <a href="/wiki/Cusco_Quechua" title="Cusco Quechua">
        Cusco Quechua
       </a>
       ; there are significant differences in other varieties of Quechua.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Quechua only has three vowel phonemes:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /u/
       </span>
       , as in Aymara (including
       <a href="/wiki/Jaqaru_language" title="Jaqaru language">
        Jaqaru
       </a>
       ). Monolingual speakers pronounce them as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [æ, ɪ, ʊ]
       </span>
       respectively, but
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       realizations
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ä, i, u]
       </span>
       may also be found. When the vowels appear adjacent to uvular consonants (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /q/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /qʼ/
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /qʰ/
       </span>
       ), they are rendered more like
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɑ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɛ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɔ]
       </span>
       , respectively.
      </p>

      <p>
       Voicing is not
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phonemic
       </a>
       in the Quechua native vocabulary of the modern Cusco variety.
      </p>
      <table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div class="center">
          <div class="floatnone">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
         <div class="haudio">
          <div style="padding:4px 0">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Qu-pata_phata_p%27ata.ogg" title="File:Qu-pata phata p'ata.ogg">
            Voiceless bilabial plosives
           </a>
          </div>
          <div>
           <div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px">
            <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="2.339410430839" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Qu-pata_phata_p'ata.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">
             <source data-bandwidth="99174" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (99 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/81/Qu-pata_phata_p%27ata.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/>

            </audio>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
           Pronunciation of the voiceless bilabial plosives of Cusco Quechua
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
         <hr/>
         <span>
          <i>
           Problems playing this file? See
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
            media help
           </a>
           .
          </i>
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       About 30% of the modern Quechua vocabulary is borrowed from Spanish, and some Spanish sounds (such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /f/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /b/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɡ/
       </span>
       ) may have become phonemic even among monolingual Quechua-speakers.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Cusco_Quechua" title="Cusco Quechua">
        Cusco Quechua
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/North_Bolivian_Quechua" title="North Bolivian Quechua">
        North-
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/South_Bolivian_Quechua" title="South Bolivian Quechua">
        South-Bolivian Quechua
       </a>
       are the only varieties of Quechua to have glottalized consonants, and they, along with certain kinds of Ecuadorian
       <a href="/wiki/Kichwa_language" title="Kichwa language">
        Kichwa
       </a>
       , are the only varieties with aspirated consonants. Because reflexes of a given Proto-Quechua word may have different stops in neighboring dialects (Proto-Quechua
       <i>
        čaki
       </i>
       'foot' becomes
       <i>
        č’aki
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        čaka
       </i>
       'bridge' becomes
       <i>
        čaka
       </i>
       ), they are thought to be innovations in Quechua from
       <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">
        Aymara
       </a>
       , borrowed independently after branching off from Proto-Quechua.
      </p>
      <p>
       Gemination of the tap
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɾ/
       </span>
       results in a trill
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [r]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">
        Stress
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Stress">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Stress is penultimate in most dialects of Quechua. In some varieties, factors such as
       <a href="/wiki/Apocope" title="Apocope">
        apocope
       </a>
       of word-final vowels may cause exceptional final stress.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">
        Orthography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Orthography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Quechua_alphabet" title="Quechua alphabet">
        Quechua alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Quechua#Standard_Quechua" title="Southern Quechua">
        Southern Quechua § Standard Quechua
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Quechua has been written using the Roman alphabet since the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Inca_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire">
        Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire
       </a>
       . However, written Quechua is rarely used by Quechua speakers because of the lack of printed referential material in Quechua.
      </p>
      <p>
       Until the 20th century, Quechua was written with a Spanish-based
       <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">
        orthography
       </a>
       . Examples
       <i>
        Inca, Huayna Cápac, Collasuyo, Mama Ocllo, Viracocha, quipu, tambo, condor
       </i>
       . That orthography is the most familiar to Spanish speakers and so has been used for most borrowings into English.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1975, the Peruvian government of
       <a href="/wiki/Juan_Velasco_Alvarado" title="Juan Velasco Alvarado">
        Juan Velasco Alvarado
       </a>
       adopted a new orthography for Quechua. That is the system preferred by the
       <a href="/wiki/Academia_Mayor_de_la_Lengua_Quechua" title="Academia Mayor de la Lengua Quechua">
        Academia Mayor de la Lengua Quechua
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Inka, Wayna Qhapaq, Qollasuyu, Mama Oqllo, Wiraqocha, khipu, tampu, kuntur
       </i>
       . The orthography has these features:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        It uses
        <b>
         w
        </b>
        instead of
        <i>
         hu
        </i>
        for
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /w/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        It distinguishes velar
        <i>
         k
        </i>
        from uvular
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        , but both were spelled
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         qu
        </i>
        in the traditional system.
       </li>
       <li>
        It distinguishes simple, ejective, and aspirated stops in dialects (such as that of the
        <a href="/wiki/Cusco_Region" title="Cusco Region">
         Cusco Region
        </a>
        ) with them, like
        <i>
         khipu
        </i>
        above.
       </li>
       <li>
        It continues to use the Spanish five-vowel system.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In 1985, a variation of this system was adopted by the Peruvian government; it uses the Quechuan three-vowel system:
       <i>
        Inka, Wayna Qhapaq, Qullasuyu, Mama Uqllu, Wiraqucha, khipu, tampu, kuntur
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The different orthographies are still highly controversial in Peru. Advocates of the traditional system believe that the new orthographies look too foreign and suggest that it makes Quechua harder to learn for people who have first been exposed to written Spanish. Those who prefer the new system maintain that it better matches the phonology of Quechua, and they point to studies showing that teaching the five-vowel system to children later causes reading difficulties in Spanish.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       For more on this, see
       <a href="/wiki/Quechuan_and_Aymaran_spelling_shift" title="Quechuan and Aymaran spelling shift">
        Quechuan and Aymaran spelling shift
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Writers differ in the treatment of Spanish loanwords. Sometimes, they are adapted to the modern orthography, and sometimes, they are left as in Spanish. For instance, "I am Roberto" could be written
       <i>
        Robertom kani
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Ruwirtum kani
       </i>
       . (The
       <i>
        -m
       </i>
       is not part of the name but is an evidential suffix. It shows how the information is known: firsthand, in this case.)
      </p>
      <p>
       The Peruvian linguist
       <a href="/wiki/Rodolfo_Cerr%C3%B3n_Palomino" title="Rodolfo Cerrón Palomino">
        Rodolfo Cerrón Palomino
       </a>
       has proposed an orthographic norm for all
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Quechua" title="Southern Quechua">
        Southern Quechua
       </a>
       : Standard Quechua (
       <i>
        el Quechua estándar
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Hanan Runasimi
       </i>
       ) conservatively integrates features of two widespread dialects,
       <a href="/wiki/Ayacucho_Quechua" title="Ayacucho Quechua">
        Ayacucho Quechua
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cusco_Quechua" title="Cusco Quechua">
        Cusco Quechua
       </a>
       . For instance:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
        <th>
         Ayacucho
        </th>
        <th>
         Cusco
        </th>
        <th>
         Standard Quechua
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <td>
         to drink
        </td>
        <td>
         upyay
        </td>
        <td>
         uhyay
        </td>
        <td>
         upyay
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <td>
         fast
        </td>
        <td>
         utqa
        </td>
        <td>
         usqha
        </td>
        <td>
         utqha
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <td>
         to work
        </td>
        <td>
         llamkay
        </td>
        <td>
         llank'ay
        </td>
        <td>
         llamk'ay
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <td>
         we (inclusive)
        </td>
        <td>
         ñuqanchik
        </td>
        <td>
         nuqanchis
        </td>
        <td>
         ñuqanchik
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <td>
         (progressive suffix)
        </td>
        <td>
         -chka-
        </td>
        <td>
         -sha-
        </td>
        <td>
         -chka-
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <td>
         day
        </td>
        <td>
         punchaw
        </td>
        <td>
         p'unchay
        </td>
        <td>
         p'unchaw
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Today the Spanish-based orthography is in conflict with the Peruvian law. According to Article 20 of
       <i>
        Decreto Supremo No 004-2016-MC
       </i>
       (Supreme Decree) which approves the Regulations to Law 29735, published in the official newspaper El Peruano on July 22, 2016, adequate spellings of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toponym" title="Toponym">
        toponyms
       </a>
       in the normalized alphabets of the indigenous languages must progressively be proposed with the aim of standardizing the namings used by the National Geographic Institute
       <i>
        (Instituto Geográfico Nacional, IGN)
       </i>
       The
       <i>
        IGN
       </i>
       realizes the necessary changes in the official maps of Peru.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decree_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-decree-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Morphological_type">
        Morphological type
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Morphological type">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Quechua is an agglutinating language. Words are built up from basic roots followed several suffixes each carrying one meaning. All varieties of Quechua are very regular
       <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">
        agglutinative languages
       </a>
       , as opposed to
       <a href="/wiki/Isolating_language" title="Isolating language">
        isolating
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">
        fusional
       </a>
       ones [Thompson]. Their normal sentence order is SOV (
       <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject–object–verb">
        subject–object–verb
       </a>
       ). Their large number of
       <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">
        suffixes
       </a>
       changes both the overall significance of words and their subtle shades of meaning. Notable grammatical features include bipersonal
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">
        conjugation
       </a>
       (verbs agree with both subject and object),
       <a href="/wiki/Evidentiality" title="Evidentiality">
        evidentiality
       </a>
       (indication of the source and veracity of knowledge), a set of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topic_(linguistics)" title="Topic (linguistics)">
        topic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">
        particles
       </a>
       , and suffixes indicating who benefits from an action and the speaker's attitude toward it, but some languages and varieties may lack some of the characteristics.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns">
        Pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table border="1" cellpadding="4" cellspacing="0" style="float:right; margin:0 0 1em 1em; background:#f9f9f9; border:1px gray solid; border-collapse:collapse; font-size:95%; vertical-align:center;">
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="background:white;border-top:2px white solid;border-right:1px solid gray;border-bottom:1px solid gray;border-left:2px white solid;">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center; background:#9fb6cd;">
         <big>
          <b>
           Number
          </b>
         </big>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:center; background:#b9d3ee;">
        <td>
         <i>
          <b>
           Singular
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <b>
           Plural
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td rowspan="3" style="background:#9FB6CD">
         <big>
          <b>
           Person
          </b>
         </big>
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; background:#b9d3ee;">
         <i>
          <b>
           First
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         Ñuqa
        </td>
        <td>
         Ñuqanchik
         <i>
          (inclusive)
         </i>
         <p>
          Ñuqayku
          <i>
           (exclusive)
          </i>
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:center; background:#b9d3ee;">
         <i>
          <b>
           Second
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         Qam
        </td>
        <td>
         Qamkuna
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:center; background:#b9d3ee;">
         <i>
          <b>
           Third
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         Pay
        </td>
        <td>
         Paykuna
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In Quechua, there are seven
       <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">
        pronouns
       </a>
       . Quechua has two first-person plural pronouns ("we" in English). One is called the
       <a href="/wiki/Clusivity" title="Clusivity">
        inclusive
       </a>
       , which is used if the speaker wishes to include the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Addressee" title="Addressee">
        addressee
       </a>
       ("we and you"). The other form is called the exclusive, which is used when the addressee is excluded ("we without you"). Quechua also adds the suffix
       <i>
        -kuna
       </i>
       to the second and third person singular pronouns
       <i>
        qam
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        pay
       </i>
       to create the plural forms,
       <i>
        qam-kuna
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        pay-kuna
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Adjectives">
        Adjectives
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Adjectives">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">
        Adjectives
       </a>
       in Quechua are always placed before nouns. They lack gender and number and are not declined to agree with
       <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">
        substantives
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers">
        Numbers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Numbers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_number_(linguistics)" title="Cardinal number (linguistics)">
         Cardinal numbers
        </a>
        .
        <i>
         ch'usaq
        </i>
        (0),
        <i>
         huk
        </i>
        (1),
        <i>
         iskay
        </i>
        (2),
        <i>
         kimsa
        </i>
        (3),
        <i>
         tawa
        </i>
        (4),
        <i>
         pichqa
        </i>
        (5),
        <i>
         suqta
        </i>
        (6),
        <i>
         qanchis
        </i>
        (7),
        <i>
         pusaq
        </i>
        (8),
        <i>
         isqun
        </i>
        (9),
        <i>
         chunka
        </i>
        (10),
        <i>
         chunka hukniyuq
        </i>
        (11),
        <i>
         chunka iskayniyuq
        </i>
        (12),
        <i>
         iskay chunka
        </i>
        (20),
        <i>
         pachak
        </i>
        (100),
        <i>
         waranqa
        </i>
        (1,000),
        <i>
         hunu
        </i>
        (1,000,000),
        <i>
         lluna
        </i>
        (1,000,000,000,000).
       </li>
       <li>
        Ordinal numbers. To form ordinal numbers, the word
        <i>
         ñiqin
        </i>
        is put after the appropriate cardinal number (
        <i>
         iskay ñiqin
        </i>
        = "second"). The only exception is that, in addition to
        <i>
         huk ñiqin
        </i>
        ("first"), the phrase
        <i>
         ñawpaq
        </i>
        is also used in the somewhat more restricted sense of "the initial, primordial, the oldest".
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">
        Nouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Nouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">
        Noun
       </a>
       roots accept suffixes that indicate
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        person
       </a>
       (defining of possession, not identity),
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">
        number
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">
        case
       </a>
       . In general, the personal suffix precedes that of number. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Santiago_del_Estero" title="Santiago del Estero">
        Santiago del Estero
       </a>
       variety, however, the order is reversed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From variety to variety, suffixes may change.
      </p>
