       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Adverbs">
        Adverbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Adverbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Adverb" title="Adverb">
        Adverbs
       </a>
       can be formed by adding
       <i>
        -ta
       </i>
       or, in some cases,
       <i>
        -lla
       </i>
       to an adjective:
       <i>
        allin – allinta
       </i>
       ("good – well"),
       <i>
        utqay – utqaylla
       </i>
       ("quick – quickly"). They are also formed by adding suffixes to
       <a href="/wiki/Demonstrative" title="Demonstrative">
        demonstratives
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        chay
       </i>
       ("that") –
       <i>
        chaypi
       </i>
       ("there"),
       <i>
        kay
       </i>
       ("this") –
       <i>
        kayman
       </i>
       ("hither").
      </p>
      <p>
       There are several original adverbs. For Europeans, it is striking that the adverb
       <i>
        qhipa
       </i>
       means both "behind" and "future" and
       <i>
        ñawpa
       </i>
       means "ahead, in front" and "past".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Local and temporal concepts of adverbs in Quechua (as well as in
       <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">
        Aymara
       </a>
       ) are associated to each other reversely, compared to European languages. For the speakers of Quechua, we are moving backwards into the future (we cannot see it: it is unknown), facing the past (we can see it: it is remembered).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">
        Verbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Verbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The infinitive forms (unconjugated) have the suffix
       <i>
        -y
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        much'a
       </i>
       = "kiss";
       <i>
        much'a-y
       </i>
       = "to kiss"). These are the endings for the indicative:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="margin:auto;">
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
         Present
        </th>
        <th>
         Past
        </th>
        <th>
         Future
        </th>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Pluperfect" title="Pluperfect">
          Pluperfect
         </a>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <th>
         Ñuqa
        </th>
        <td>
         -ni
        </td>
        <td>
         -rqa-ni
        </td>
        <td>
         -saq
        </td>
        <td>
         -sqa-ni
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <th>
         Qam
        </th>
        <td>
         -nki
        </td>
        <td>
         -rqa-nki
        </td>
        <td>
         -nki
        </td>
        <td>
         -sqa-nki
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <th>
         Pay
        </th>
        <td>
         -n
        </td>
        <td>
         -rqa(-n)
        </td>
        <td>
         -nqa
        </td>
        <td>
         -sqa
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <th>
         Ñuqanchik
        </th>
        <td>
         -nchik
        </td>
        <td>
         -rqa-nchik
        </td>
        <td>
         -su-nchik
        </td>
        <td>
         -sqa-nchik
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <th>
         Ñuqayku
        </th>
        <td>
         -yku
        </td>
        <td>
         -rqa-yku
        </td>
        <td>
         -saq-ku
        </td>
        <td>
         -sqa-yku
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <th>
         Qamkuna
        </th>
        <td>
         -nki-chik
        </td>
        <td>
         -rqa-nki-chik
        </td>
        <td>
         -nki-chik
        </td>
        <td>
         -sqa-nki-chik
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background:#f0f8ff;">
        <th>
         Paykuna
        </th>
        <td>
         -n-ku
        </td>
        <td>
         -rqa-(n)ku
        </td>
        <td>
         -nqa-ku
        </td>
        <td>
         -sqa-ku
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The suffixes shown in the table above usually indicate the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_subject" title="Grammatical subject">
        subject
       </a>
       ; the person of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_object" title="Grammatical object">
        object
       </a>
       is also indicated by a suffix (
       <i>
        -a-
       </i>
       for first person and
       <i>
        -su-
       </i>
       for second person), which precedes the suffixes in the table. In such cases, the plural suffixes from the table (
       <i>
        -chik
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        -ku
       </i>
       ) can be used to express the number of the object rather than the subject.
      </p>
      <p>
       Various suffixes are added to the stem to change the meaning. For example,
       <i>
        -chi
       </i>
       is a causative and
       <i>
        -ku
       </i>
       is a reflexive (example:
       <i>
        wañuy
       </i>
       = "to die";
       <i>
        wañuchiy
       </i>
       = to kill
       <i>
        wañuchikuy
       </i>
       = "to commit suicide");
       <i>
        -naku
       </i>
       is used for mutual action (example:
       <i>
        marq'ay
       </i>
       = "to hug";
       <i>
        marq'anakuy
       </i>
       = "to hug each other"), and
       <i>
        -chka
       </i>
       is a progressive, used for an ongoing action (e.g.,
       <i>
        mikhuy
       </i>
       = "to eat";
       <i>
        mikhuchkay
       </i>
       = "to be eating").
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammatical_particles">
        Grammatical particles
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Grammatical particles">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">
        Particles
       </a>
       are indeclinable: they do not accept suffixes. They are relatively rare, but the most common are
       <i>
        arí
       </i>
       ("yes") and
       <i>
        mana
       </i>
       ("no"), although
       <i>
        mana
       </i>
       can take some suffixes, such as
       <i>
        -n
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        -m
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        manan
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        manam
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        -raq
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        manaraq
       </i>
       , not yet) and
       <i>
        -chu
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        manachu?
       </i>
       , or not?), to intensify the meaning. Also used are
       <i>
        yaw
       </i>
       ("hey", "hi"), and certain loan words from Spanish, such as
       <i>
        piru
       </i>
       (from Spanish
       <i>
        pero
       </i>
       "but") and
       <i>
        sinuqa
       </i>
       (from
       <i>
        sino
       </i>
       "rather").
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Evidentiality">
        Evidentiality
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Evidentiality">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Quechuan languages have three different morphemes that mark
       <a href="/wiki/Evidentiality" title="Evidentiality">
        evidentiality
       </a>
       . Evidentiality refers to a morpheme whose primary purpose is to indicate the source of information.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the Quechuan languages, evidentiality is a three-term system: there are three evidential morphemes that mark varying levels of source information. The markers can apply to the first, second, and third persons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aikhenvald_2004,_p._377_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Aikhenvald_2004,_p._377-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The chart below depicts an example of these morphemes from
       <a href="/wiki/Wanka_Quechua" title="Wanka Quechua">
        Wanka Quechua
       </a>
       :
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <td>
         Evidential morphemes
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          -m(i)
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          -chr(a)
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          -sh(i)
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Meaning
        </td>
        <td>
         Direct evidence
        </td>
        <td>
         Inferred; conjecture
        </td>
        <td>
         Reported; hearsay
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The parentheses around the vowels indicate that the vowel can be dropped in when following an open vowel. For the sake of cohesiveness, the above forms are used to discuss the evidential morphemes. However, it should be noted that there are dialectal variations to the forms. The variations will be presented in the following descriptions.
      </p>
      <p>
       The following sentences provide examples of the three evidentials and further discuss the meaning behind each of them.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="-m.28i.29_:_Direct_evidence_and_commitment.5B30.5D">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="-m(i)_:_Direct_evidence_and_commitment[30]">
        <i>
         -m(i)
        </i>
        : Direct evidence and commitment
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-30">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: -m(i) : Direct evidence and commitment[30]">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Regional variations: In
       <a href="/wiki/Cusco_Quechua" title="Cusco Quechua">
        Cusco Quechua
       </a>
       , the direct evidential presents itself as
       <i>
        –mi
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        –n
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The evidential
       <i>
        –mi
       </i>
       indicates that the speaker has a "strong personal conviction the veracity of the circumstance expressed."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has the basis of direct personal experience.
      </p>
      <p>
       Wanka Quechua
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          ñawi-i-wan-
          <b>
           mi
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          lika-la-a
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         eye-
         <small>
          1P
         </small>
         -with-
         <small>
          DIR
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         see-
         <small>
          PST
         </small>
         -1
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       I saw them with my own eyes.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="-chr.28a.29_:_Inference_and_attenuation.5B33.5D">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="-chr(a)_:_Inference_and_attenuation[33]">
        <i>
         -chr(a)
        </i>
        : Inference and attenuation
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
         <a href="#cite_note-33">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: -chr(a) : Inference and attenuation[33]">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In Quechuan languages, not specified by the source, the inference morpheme appears as
       <i>
        –ch(i), -ch(a), -chr(a)
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        –chr(a)
       </i>
       evidential indicates that the utterance is an inference or form of conjecture. That inference relays the speaker’s non-commitment to the truth-value of the statement. It also appears in cases such as acquiescence, irony, interrogative constructions, and first person inferences. These uses constitute nonprototypical use and will be later discussed in the
       <i>
        changes in meaning and other uses
       </i>
       section.
      </p>
      <p>
       Wanka Quechua
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          kuti-mu-n'a-qa-
          <b>
           chr
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ni-ya-ami
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         return-
         <small>
          AFAR
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          3FUT
         </small>
         -now-
         <small>
          CONJ
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         say-
         <small>
          IMPV
         </small>
         -1-
         <small>
          DIR
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       I think they will probably come back.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="-sh.28i.29_:_Hearsay.5B35.5D">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="-sh(i)_:_Hearsay[35]">
        <i>
         -sh(i)
        </i>
        : Hearsay
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
         <a href="#cite_note-35">
          [35]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: -sh(i) : Hearsay[35]">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Regional variations: It can appear as
       <i>
        –sh(i)
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        –s(i)
       </i>
       depending on the dialect.
      </p>
      <p>
       With the use of this morpheme, the speaker "serves as a conduit through which information from another source passes." The information being related is hearsay or revelatory in nature. It also works to express the uncertainty of the speaker regarding the situation. However, it also appears in other constructions that are discussed in the
       <i>
        changes in meaning
       </i>
       section.
      </p>
      <p>
       Wanka Quechua
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          shanti-
          <b>
           sh
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          prista-ka-mu-la
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Shanti-
         <small>
          REP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         borrow-
         <small>
          REF
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          AFAR
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          PST
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       (I was told) Shanti borrowed it.
      </p>
      <p>
       Hintz discusses an interesting case of evidential behavior found in the Sihaus dialect of
       <a href="/wiki/Ancash_Quechua" title="Ancash Quechua">
        Ancash Quechua
       </a>
       . The author postulates that instead of three single evidential markers, that Quechuan language contains three pairs of evidential markers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Affix_or_clitic">
        Affix or clitic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Affix or clitic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       It may have been noted the evidential morphemes have been referred to as markers or morphemes. The literature seems to differ on whether or not the evidential morphemes are acting as affixes or clitics, in some cases, such as Wanka Quechua, enclitics. Lefebvre and Muysken (1998) discuss this issue in terms of case but remark the line between affix and clitic is not clear.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both terms are used interchangeably throughout these sections.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Position_in_the_sentence">
        Position in the sentence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Position in the sentence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Evidentials in the Quechuan languages are "second position enclitics", which usually attach to the first constituent in the sentence, as shown in this example.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          huk-
          <b>
           si
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ka-sqa
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          huk
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          machucha-piwan
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          payacha
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         once-
         <small>
          REP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         be-
         <small>
          SD
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         one
        </td>
        <td>
         old.man-
         <small>
          WITH
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         woman
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Once, there were an old man and an old woman.
      </p>
      <p>
       They can, however, also occur on a focused constituent.
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          Pidru
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kunana-
          <b>
           mi
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          wasi-ta
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          tuwa-sha-n
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Pedro
        </td>
        <td>
         now-
         <small>
          DIR.EV
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         house-
         <small>
          ACC
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         build-
         <small>
          PROG
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          3SG
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       It is now that Pedro is building the house.
      </p>
      <p>
       Sometimes, the affix is described as attaching to the focus, particularly in the Tarma dialect of
       <a href="/wiki/Yaru_Quechua" title="Yaru Quechua">
        Yaru Quechua
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but this does not hold true for all varieties of Quechua. In Huanuco Quechua, the evidentials may follow any number of topics, marked by the topic marker
       <i>
        –qa
       </i>
       , and the element with the evidential must precede the main verb or be the main verb.
      </p>
      <p>
       However, there are exceptions to that rule, and the more topics there are in a sentence, the more likely the sentence is to deviate from the usual pattern.
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          Chawrana-qa
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          puntataruu-qu
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          trayaruptin-qa
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          wamrata-qa
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          mayna-
          <b>
           shi
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Diosninchi-qa
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          heqarkaykachisha
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          syelutana-shi
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         so:already-
         <small>
          TOP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         at:the:peak-
         <small>
          TOP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         arriving-
         <small>
          TOP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         child-
         <small>
          TOP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         already-
         <small>
          IND
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         our:God-
         <small>
          TOP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         had:taken:her:up
        </td>
        <td>
         to:heaven:already-
         <small>
          IND
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       When she (the witch) reached the peak, God had already taken the child up into heaven.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_meaning_and_other_uses">
        Changes in meaning and other uses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Changes in meaning and other uses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Evidentials can be used to relay different meanings depending on the context and perform other functions. The following examples are restricted to Wanka Quechua.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        <b>
         The direct evidential, -mi
        </b>
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       The direct evidential appears in wh-questions and yes/no questions. By considering the direct evidential in terms of prototypical semantics, it seems somewhat counterintuitive to have a direct evidential, basically an evidential that confirms the speaker’s certainty about a topic, in a question. However, if one focuses less on the structure and more on the situation, some sense can be made. The speaker is asking the addressee for information so the speaker assumes the speaker knows the answer. That assumption is where the direct evidential comes into play. The speaker holds a certain amount of certainty that the addressee will know the answer. The speaker interprets the addressee as being in "direct relation" to the proposed content; the situation is the same as when, in regular sentences, the speaker assumes direct relation to the proposed information.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          imay-
          <b>
           mi
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          wankayuu-pu
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kuti-mu-la
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         when-
         <small>
          DIR
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         Huancayo-
         <small>
          ABL
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         return-
         <small>
          AFAR
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          PAST
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       When did he come back from Huancayo?
      </p>
      <p>
       (Floyd 1999, p. 85)
      </p>
      <p>
       The direct evidential affix is also seen in yes/no questions, similar to the situation with wh-questions. Floyd describes yes/no questions as being "characterized as instructions to the addressee to assert one of the propositions of a disjunction."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Once again, the burden of direct evidence is being placed on the addressee, not on the speaker. The question marker in Wanka Quechua,
       <i>
        -chun
       </i>
       , is derived from the negative –chu marker and the direct evidential (realized as –n in some dialects).
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          tarma-kta
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          li-n-
          <b>
           chun
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Tarma-
         <small>
          ACC
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         go-
         <small>
          3
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          YN
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Is he going to Tarma?
      </p>
      <p>
       (Floyd 1999, p. 89)
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="The_inferential_evidential.2C_-chr.28a.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_inferential_evidential,_-chr(a)">
        The inferential evidential, -chr(a)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: The inferential evidential, -chr(a)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       While
       <i>
        –chr(a)
       </i>
       is usually used in an inferential context, it has some non-prototypical uses.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Mild Exhortation
       </i>
       In these constructions the evidential works to reaffirm and encourage the addressee’s actions or thoughts.
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          mas
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kalu-kuna-kta
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          li-la-a
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ni-nki-
          <b>
           chra
          </b>
          -ri
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         more
        </td>
        <td>
         far-
         <small>
          PL
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          ACC
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         go-
         <small>
          PST
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          1
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         say-
         <small>
          2
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          CONJ
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          EMPH
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Yes, tell them, "I've gone farther."
      </p>
      <p>
       (Floyd 1999, p. 107)
      </p>
      <p>
       This example comes from a conversation between husband and wife, discussing the reactions of their family and friends after they have been gone for a while. The husband says he plans to stretch the truth and tell them about distant places to which he has gone, and his wife (in the example above) echoes and encourages his thoughts.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Acquiescence
       </i>
       With these, the evidential is used to highlight the speaker’s assessment of inevitability of an event and acceptance of it. There is a sense of resistance, diminished enthusiasm, and disinclination in these constructions.
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          paaga-lla-shrayki-
          <b>
           chra
          </b>
          -a
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         pay-
         <small>
          POL
         </small>
         -1›2
         <small>
          FUT
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          CONJ
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          EMPH
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       I suppose I'll pay you then.
      </p>
      <p>
       (Floyd 1999, p. 109)
      </p>
      <p>
       This example comes from a discourse where a woman demands compensation from the man (the speaker in the example) whose pigs ruined her potatoes. He denies the pigs as being his but finally realizes he may be responsible and produces the above example.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Interrogative
       </i>
       Somewhat similar to the
       <i>
        –mi
       </i>
       evidential, the inferential evidential can be found in content questions. However, the salient difference between the uses of the evidentials in questions is that in the
       <i>
        –m(i)
       </i>
       marked questions, an answer is expected. That is not the case with
       <i>
        –chr(a)
       </i>
       marked questions.
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          ima-lla-kta-
          <b>
           chr
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          u-you-shrun
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          llapa
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ayllu-kuna-kta-si
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          chra-alu-l
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         what-
         <small>
          LIM
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          ACC
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          CONJ
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         give-
         <small>
          ASP
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          12FUT
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         all
        </td>
        <td>
         family-
         <small>
          PL
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          ACC
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          EVEN
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         arrive-
         <small>
          ASP
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          SS
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       I wonder what we will give our families when we arrive.
      </p>
      <p>
       (Floyd 1999, p. 111)
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Irony
       </i>
       Irony in language can be a somewhat complicated topic in how it functions differently in languages, and by its semantic nature, it is already somewhat vague. For these purposes, it is suffice to say that when irony takes place in Wanka Quechua, the
       <i>
        –chr(a)
       </i>
       marker is used.
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          chay-nuu-pa-
          <b>
           chr
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          yachra-nki
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         that-
         <small>
          SIM
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          GEN
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          CONJ
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         know-
         <small>
          2
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       (I suppose) That's how you learn [that is the way in which you will learn].
      </p>
      <p>
       (Floyd 199, p. 115)
      </p>
      <p>
       This example comes from discourse between a father and daughter about her refusal to attend school. It can be interpreted as a genuine statement (perhaps one can learn by resisting school) or as an ironic statement (that is an absurd idea).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Hearsay_evidential.2C_-sh.28i.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hearsay_evidential,_-sh(i)">
        Hearsay evidential, -sh(i)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Hearsay evidential, -sh(i)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Aside from being used to express hearsay and revelation, this affix also has other uses.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Folktales, myths, and legends
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       Because folktales, myths, and legends are, in essence, reported speech, it follows that the hearsay marker would be used with them. Many of these types of stories are passed down through generations, furthering this aspect of reported speech. A difference between simple hearsay and folktales can be seen in the frequency of the
       <i>
        –sh(i)
       </i>
       marker. In normal conversation using reported speech, the marker is used less, to avoid redundancy.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Riddles
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       Riddles are somewhat similar to myths and folktales in that their nature is to be passed by word of mouth.
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          ima-lla-
          <b>
           shi
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          ayka-lla-
          <b>
           sh
          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          juk
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          machray-chru
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          puñu-ya-n
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          puka
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          waaka
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         what-
         <small>
          LIM
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          REP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         how^much-
         <small>
          LIM
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          REP
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         one
        </td>
        <td>
         cave-
         <small>
          LOC
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         sleep-
         <small>
          IMPF
         </small>
         -
         <small>
          3
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         red
        </td>
        <td>
         cow
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       (Floyd 1999, p. 142)
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Omission_and_overuse_of_evidential_affixes">
        Omission and overuse of evidential affixes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Omission and overuse of evidential affixes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In certain grammatical structures, the evidential marker does not appear at all. In all Quechuan languages the evidential will not appear in a dependent clause. Sadly, no example was given to depict this omission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Omissions occur in Quechua. The sentence is understood to have the same evidentiality as the other sentences in the context. Quechuan speakers vary as to how much they omit evidentials, but they occur only in connected speech.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An interesting contrast to omission of evidentials is overuse of evidentials. If a speaker uses evidentials too much with no reason, competence is brought into question. For example, the overuse of –m(i) could lead others to believe that the speaker is not a native speaker or, in some extreme cases, that one is mentally ill.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aikhenvald_2004,_p._377_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Aikhenvald_2004,_p._377-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_aspect">
        Cultural aspect
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Cultural aspect">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       By using evidentials, the Quechua culture has certain assumptions about the information being relayed. Those who do not abide by the cultural customs should not be trusted. A passage from Weber (1986) summarizes them nicely below:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        (Only) one’s experience is reliable.
       </li>
       <li>
        Avoid unnecessary risk by assuming responsibility for information of which one is not absolutely certain.
       </li>
       <li>
        Do not be gullible. There are many folktales in which the villain is foiled by his gullibility.
       </li>
       <li>
        Assume responsibility only if it is safe to do so. Successful assumption of responsibility builds stature in the community.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Evidentials also show that being precise and stating the source of one’s information is extremely important in the language and the culture. Failure to use them correctly can lead to diminished standing in the community. Speakers are aware of the evidentials and even use proverbs to teach children the importance of being precise and truthful. Precision and information source are of the utmost importance. They are a powerful and resourceful method of human communication.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
        Literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Although the body of literature in Quechua is not as sizable as its historical and current prominence would suggest, it is nevertheless not negligible.
      </p>
      <p>
       As in the case of the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_pre-Columbian_cultures" title="List of pre-Columbian cultures">
        pre-Columbian Mesoamerica
       </a>
       , there are a number of surviving Andean documents in the local language that were written down in Latin characters after the European conquest, but they express, to a great extent, the culture of pre-Conquest times. That type of Quechua literature is somewhat scantier, but nevertheless significant. It includes the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/Huarochir%C3%AD_Manuscript" title="Huarochirí Manuscript">
        Huarochirí Manuscript
       </a>
       (1598), describing the mythology and religion of the valley of
       <a href="/wiki/Huarochir%C3%AD_Province" title="Huarochirí Province">
        Huarochirí
       </a>
       as well as Quechua poems quoted within the Spanish-language texts of some chronicles dealing with the pre-Conquest period. There are a number of anonymous or signed Quechua dramas dating from the post-conquest period (starting from the 17th century), some of which deal with the Inca era, while most are on religious topics and of European inspiration. The most famous dramas is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ollantay" title="Ollantay">
         Ollantay
        </a>
       </i>
       and the plays describing the death of
       <a href="/wiki/Atahualpa" title="Atahualpa">
        Atahualpa
       </a>
       . For example,
       <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_Espinosa_Medrano" title="Juan de Espinosa Medrano">
        Juan de Espinosa Medrano
       </a>
       wrote several dramas in the language. Poems in Quechua were also composed during the colonial period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelaar254_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelaar254-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Dramas and poems continued to be written in the 19th and especially in 20th centuries as well; in addition, in the 20th century and more recently, more prose has been published. However, few literary forms were made present in the 19th century as European influences limited literary criticism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While some of that literature consists of original compositions (poems and dramas), the bulk of 20th century Quechua literature consists of traditional folk stories and oral narratives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelaar254_47-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelaar254-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Payne" title="Johnny Payne">
        Johnny Payne
       </a>
       has translated two sets of Quechua oral short stories, one into Spanish and the other into English.
      </p>
      <p>
       Many Andean musicians write and sing in their native languages, including Quechua and Aymara. Notable musical groups are
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Kjarkas" title="Los Kjarkas">
        Los Kjarkas
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kala_Marka&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kala Marka (page does not exist)">
        Kala Marka
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=J%27acha_Mallku&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="J'acha Mallku (page does not exist)">
        J'acha Mallku
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Savia_Andina" title="Savia Andina">
        Savia Andina
       </a>
       , Wayna Picchu, Wara and many others.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">
        In popular culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Quechuan_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: In popular culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <ul>
       <li>
        There are several Quechua and Quechua-Spanish bloggers, as well as a Quechua language podcast.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The 1961 Peruvian film
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Kukuli" title="Kukuli">
          Kukuli
         </a>
        </i>
        was the first film to be spoken in the Quechua language.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latinosinlondon_51-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-latinosinlondon-51">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        In
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">
          Star Wars Episode IV: A New Hope
         </a>
        </i>
        the character
        <a href="/wiki/Greedo" title="Greedo">
         Greedo
        </a>
        's native language, Rodian, is actually a simplified version of Quechua, which is shown in conversation with
        <a href="/wiki/Han_Solo" title="Han Solo">
         Han Solo
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The '90s TV series
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Sentinel_(TV_series)" title="The Sentinel (TV series)">
          The Sentinel
         </a>
        </i>
        included numerous references to the shamanism and spirituality of the Peruvian Chopec as well as including many Quechua words in several episodes.
       </li>
       <li>
        The sport retailer
        <a href="/wiki/Decathlon_Group" title="Decathlon Group">
         Decathlon Group
        </a>
        brands their mountain equipment range as
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Quechua_(brand)" title="Quechua (brand)">
          Quechua
         </a>
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        In
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Jones_and_the_Kingdom_of_the_Crystal_Skull" title="Indiana Jones and the Kingdom of the Crystal Skull">
          Indiana Jones and the Kingdom of the Crystal Skull
         </a>
        </i>
        , Indy has a dialogue in Quechua with Peruvians. He explains he learned the language in Mexico from a couple of the "guys" he met while briefly riding with
        <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Villa" title="Pancho Villa">
         Pancho Villa
        </a>
        . This adventure was featured in the pilot episode of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Young_Indiana_Jones_Chronicles" title="The Young Indiana Jones Chronicles">
          The Young Indiana Jones Chronicles
         </a>
        </i>
        . The guys were most likely Peruvian mercenaries recruited to the
        <a href="/wiki/Divisi%C3%B3n_del_Norte" title="División del Norte">
         División del Norte
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        In
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">
          The Adventures of Tintin
         </a>
        </i>
        books
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Seven_Crystal_Balls" title="The Seven Crystal Balls">
          The Seven Crystal Balls
         </a>
        </i>
        and its sequel
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_the_Sun" title="Prisoners of the Sun">
          Prisoners of the Sun
         </a>
        </i>
        , there are Quechua characters who are in league with the Inca and facilitate the abduction and incarceration of
        <a href="/wiki/Professor_Calculus" title="Professor Calculus">
         Professor Calculus
        </a>
        at the Temple of the Sun for committing sacrilege by wearing the funerary bangle of
        <a href="/wiki/List_of_The_Adventures_of_Tintin_characters#Rascar_Capac" title="List of The Adventures of Tintin characters">
         Rascar Capac
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        In the
        <a href="/wiki/Collectible_card_game" title="Collectible card game">
         collectible card game
        </a>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Yu-Gi-Oh!" title="Yu-Gi-Oh!">
          Yu-Gi-Oh!
         </a>
        </i>
        , monsters in the card series
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_Yu-Gi-Oh!_cards&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="List of Yu-Gi-Oh! cards (page does not exist)">
         Earthbound Immortals
        </a>
        have their name originated from Quechua. In
        <a href="/wiki/Yu-Gi-Oh!_5D%27s" title="Yu-Gi-Oh! 5D's">
         the animated series
        </a>
        , Earthbound Immortals are described as powerful beasts sealed in
        <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Lines" title="Nazca Lines">
         Nazca Lines
        </a>
        , which each one of them represents.
       </li>
       <li>
        On the TV cartoon series
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_New_School" title="The Emperor's New School">
          The Emperor's New School
         </a>
        </i>
        , the main and other characters have Quechua names as
        <i>
         Kuzco
        </i>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">
         Cusco
        </a>
        , that means "navel of the world"),
        <i>
         Pacha
        </i>
        (ground) and
        <i>
         Chicha
        </i>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Chicha" title="Chicha">
         chicha
        </a>
        is a kind of fermented maize "beer").
       </li>
       <li>
        In
        <a href="/wiki/Da_Vinci%27s_Demons" title="Da Vinci's Demons">
         Da Vinci's Demons
        </a>
        , season 2 they meet a group of "Indians" who speak this language
       </li>
       <li>
        In Calle 13's song, Latin America (Grammy winner 2011 - the beginning there is a DJ introducing Calle 13 and the singer responds back in Quechuan, "this one is for you all" before starting)
       </li>
       <li>
        Ruanja, a language spoken in Mary Doria Russel's
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Sparrow_(novel)" title="The Sparrow (novel)">
          The Sparrow
         </a>
        </i>
        is based on Quechua.
       </li>
      </ul>

           <a href="#cite_ref-Andes_13-0">

           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Borsdorf, Axel.
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=lgEyBwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA142&amp;lpg=PA142&amp;dq=ecuador+constitution+quechua&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=8GozrigzHx&amp;sig=JpuKLTOIIyBexrmjlEPPMid49vY&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;sqi=2&amp;ved=0ahUKEwj8o7SJtKHMAhWEvoMKHTC1BHYQ6AEIWjAN#v=onepage&amp;q=ecuador%20constitution%20quechua&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             The Andes: A Geographical Portrait
            </i>
           </a>
           . p. 142.
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Andes%3A+A+Geographical+Portrait&amp;rft.pages=142&amp;rft.aulast=Borsdorf&amp;rft.aufirst=Axel&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DlgEyBwAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA142%26lpg%3DPA142%26dq%3Decuador%2Bconstitution%2Bquechua%26source%3Dbl%26ots%3D8GozrigzHx%26sig%3DJpuKLTOIIyBexrmjlEPPMid49vY%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26sqi%3D2%26ved%3D0ahUKEwj8o7SJtKHMAhWEvoMKHTC1BHYQ6AEIWjAN%23v%3Donepage%26q%3Decuador%2520constitution%2520quechua%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AQuechuan+languages">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-14">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-14">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Adelaar 2004, pp. 258–259: "The Quechua speakers' wish for social mobility for their children is often heard as an argument for not transmitting the language to the next generation.... As observed quite adequately by
          <a href="/wiki/Rodolfo_Cerr%C3%B3n_Palomino" title="Rodolfo Cerrón Palomino">
           Cerrón Palomino
          </a>
          , "Quechua (and
          <a href="/wiki/Aymaran_languages" title="Aymaran languages">
           Aymara
          </a>
          ) speakers seem to have taken the project of assimilation begun by the dominating classes and made it their own."
         </span>
